  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_af3d9c7e-8abc-4aec-a7f5-68503839bef9'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6471719991079729923">Since its publication in 1977, Stephen Donaldson's award-winning trilogy has become an indisputable fantasy class, hailed by the critics and loved by millions of readers around the world. Now all three books are available in one paperback volume for the first time. <br/><br/>Thomas Covenant, an embittered and cynical writer, afflicted with leprosy and shunned by society, is fated to</span>
  <span id="freeText6471719991079729923" style="display:none">Since its publication in 1977, Stephen Donaldson's award-winning trilogy has become an indisputable fantasy class, hailed by the critics and loved by millions of readers around the world. Now all three books are available in one paperback volume for the first time. <br /><br />Thomas Covenant, an embittered and cynical writer, afflicted with leprosy and shunned by society, is fated to become the heroic savior of The Land, an alternate world. In the novels, he struggles against the satanic Lord Foul, "The Despiser", who intends to escape the bondage of the physical universe and wreak revenge upon his arch-enemy, "The Creator".<br /><br />Stephen R. Donaldson's works are infused with psychological undertones involving an exploration of the darker side of the protagonist Thomas Covenant whilst preserving strong humanist ideals. The contextual richness of the Land's varied geography, races, cultures and history enables all three series of the Chronicles to explore and expand upon an increasingly diverse and storied environment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6471719991079729923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1071997-when-i-first-read-the-chronicles-of-thomas">When I first read The Chronicles of Thomas Covenant (The First 6 Volumes) I was transported, elevated even. Almost 40 years later, as I re-read them, the final trilogy, and capstone, I still enjoy the story, but often find his use of language pretentious and tedious, even. Anybody else with me here?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1071997'><a id="like_count_community_question_1071997" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1071997?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F179033.The_Chronicles_of_Thomas_Covenant_the_Unbeliever">like</a></span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/37380769-rebecca-terry"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1417126818i/37380769._UX100_CR0,5,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/37380769-rebecca-terry">Rebecca Terry</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I am so glad it is not just e. I first read these when I was 14 and absolutely loved them. I reread them around 4 times in 4 years. I have had them on<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I am so glad it is not just e. I first read these when I was 14 and absolutely loved them. I reread them around 4 times in 4 years. I have had them on my shelf ever since and finally got around to rereading decades later. I am struggling through book 2 at the moment, the language reads like my 14 yr old self's attempts at making people sound important, the 'stirring speeches' are frequently cringeworthy in the extreme. Let's take this example, this is said by people who are basically the premier academics and loremasters of the entire world on greeting TC "Hail, white gold wielder! - you who are named ur-Lord Thomas Covenant, Unbeliever and Ringthane" <br />Ouch, even at 14 I would have known that was shudderingly bad.<br /><br />Many of the interactions with TC follow this type of pattern which actually undermines the central question of the book, whether the Land is real or a dream. If it is real then people don't speak like this, if it is a dream then of course they will react as if TC is a demigod/messiah figure. <br /><br />There is a heavy religious overtone to the way everyone reacts to TC which isn't supported by the characterisation of the land and its people either. There is no organised religion and not everyone believes in the myth of the creator even, but they behave like religious fanatics - compltee with clunky prose and overly earnest motivation. They often behave as if members of some hippy cult, wafting around in robes, worshipping trees, having these long drawn out celebrations and group hug gatherings. Even the famed hunters of the plains worship horses and wear flowers in their hair. <br /><br />The issue of the challanging actions of TC seems to get glossed over as well. I admire SD idea to include it, and at first it seems to really bring out the crises and challange in the concept of the unbeliever. THis is a pretty crappy excuse for a human being, he is rude, distrustful, sneering and a rapist. And yet, everyone basically adores him and forgives the brutal rape of a 16 yr old girl by a man over twice her age. As an adult I become profoundly uncomfortable not only with the way casual characters respond to TC, but specifically by male characters (other than Troy) who know the full story. Lord Mhoram and Saltheart Foamfollow are the two worst examples. They are both in different ways held up as ideal males, people we are supposed to like and respect. And yet both of them refuse to censure the actions of a rapist. <br />TC emotional turmoil is deemed a good enough excuse for raping a girl.<br /><br />To make it worse, in the second book his daughter of that rape, knowing exactly what he did, seduces him. The whole series is a male fantasy, rape without consequences, seduction by a powerful and beautiful woman who wants you so badly they will break social taboos for you, people heaping praise and treasure on you even when you are rude. Its just plain boring after a while.<br /><a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<a href="/questions/936139-what-age-group-is-this-for---could-a-young">what age group is this for - could a young teen who is an avid reader be OK, is there anything really disturbing in it? Not being a fantasy reader myself I am unsure as to how much adult content there may be.</a>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/6314298-sandra-roberts"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/6314298-sandra-roberts">Sandra Roberts</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The book deals with some rather difficult subject matter, both physical and social. I would say it's fine for a mature reader, I read it when I was yo<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The book deals with some rather difficult subject matter, both physical and social. I would say it's fine for a mature reader, I read it when I was young but my comprehensions skills were high as was my knowledge concerning social interactions and relationships. I would say this is better suited to young adults<br />I suggest the Xanth Series by Piers Anthony for young teens a never ending series of books (I think there are over 30 now) Fantasy based on Puns which are very entertaining for young readers and without adult content for parents to worry about. Also Incarnations of Immortality by Piers Anthony is excellent and his "Adept Series" seems great for boys my sons loved them <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_82136660" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82136660" />
    <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin"><img alt="Mike (the Paladin)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528431476p2/2187043.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82136660?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike (the Paladin)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike (the Paladin)" href="/user/show/2187043-mike-the-paladin">Mike (the Paladin)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2187043-mike-the-paladin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82136660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6904408021529471287">SAVE YOURSELF!<br/><br/>Run...run fast and run far don't get caught in this book! I had to finish the first trilogy (for some reason) once I started, and I was miserable! I picked up the first book in the second trilogy and it was again "Oh woe is me. My life is awful, I dare not believe anything good. I will make everyone around me miserable. I will bemoan my fate constantly no matter what...." I threw it away. A friend recommended another completely different series of books by Mr. Donaldson and said "o</span>
  <span id="freeText6904408021529471287" style="display:none">SAVE YOURSELF!<br /><br />Run...run fast and run far don't get caught in this book! I had to finish the first trilogy (for some reason) once I started, and I was miserable! I picked up the first book in the second trilogy and it was again "Oh woe is me. My life is awful, I dare not believe anything good. I will make everyone around me miserable. I will bemoan my fate constantly no matter what...." I threw it away. A friend recommended another completely different series of books by Mr. Donaldson and said "oh no it's not like that." I tried it. A female protagonist starts the first book with "Oh woe is me. I'm so miserable. Life is awful. I will spread my misery..." I flung it from me and now avoid this writer like the plague.<br /><br />Thomas Covenant is the world's most miserable, gloomy, wretched, irritating, hateful, abhorrent, despicable, contemptible, disheartening, forlorn, self pitying excuse for a (make believe) human being it's ever been my misfortune to read about. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ When he ends up raping the one woman willing to help him so as to prove none of this is real <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> I was pretty much done with him. I read this trilogy with a group of friends so finished them. I'm more careful about what I commit to in group reads now.<br /><br />Do you get the idea? I HATE these books. if you don't, well good for you. But you need a good, really good attitude to read this tomb errr, I mean tome, consisting of Thomas Covenant's self centered misery.<br /><br />Maybe you could consider a therapist?????<br /><br />I reviewed this a while back, but I want to update it. I want to include a quote from Dorothy Parker I've used before: <br /><br /><i>
  <u>
    <b>"This is not a novel to be tossed aside lightly. It should be thrown with great force."</b>
  </u>
</i></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_4681430" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4681430" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63444-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187495654p2/63444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4681430?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/63444-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345329622" href="/book/show/817177.The_Chronicles_of_Thomas_Covenant_the_Unbeliever">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">existentialists, crossover fantasy fans, almost any fantasy fan</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=sf-f-h">sf-f-h</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63444-seth?shelf=masterpieces">masterpieces</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4681430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4492091834359006514">This series is somewhat infamous: it's widely regarded as brilliant (which it is), it's widely considered depressing (which it can be), the hero is often unappealing (which is the point), and many find the trilogy at least 25% too long (which is true). Plus, the follow-on trilogy tells almost the same story with almost the same point to it.<br/><br/>So, what's the fuss about?<br/><br/><i>Covenant</i> isn't "Tolkien with the serial numbers filed off." That it holds together with a complete fantasy story in a clear, magical</span>
  <span id="freeText4492091834359006514" style="display:none">This series is somewhat infamous: it's widely regarded as brilliant (which it is), it's widely considered depressing (which it can be), the hero is often unappealing (which is the point), and many find the trilogy at least 25% too long (which is true). Plus, the follow-on trilogy tells almost the same story with almost the same point to it.<br /><br />So, what's the fuss about?<br /><br /><i>Covenant</i> isn't "Tolkien with the serial numbers filed off." That it holds together with a complete fantasy story in a clear, magical fantasy world and you never once want to compare it to Middle Earth is a good enough start to recommend reading it. Covenant is a fantasy <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger" title="The Stranger by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stranger</a>, taking a disaffected character who denies all responsibility in his life and feels completely disconnected from the world around him and giving him the power of life and death. <br /><br />The character Thomas Covenant is definitely unappealing. I put the book down and had to restart it 3 times after a scene in the first book where he--refusing to believe that the woman he was with was anything other than a dream--committed a rape. The morality of how you treat not-real people is a regular theme of Donaldson's and this is his first and harshest demonstration of the subject. It's not easy to read and it's not easy to go on with a protagonist like that.<br /><br />Watching him slowly learn guilt, remorse, and repentence takes the next two-and-a-half books. As many people comment, that portion of the story is a downer. <br /><br />Alongside the moral tale we have the fantasy epic: a modern American man returning to a fantasy world (The Land) several times seeing the consequences of his involvement in their epic battle against their own satan-figure: Lord Foul, who wants to break the Arch of the World and release himself into our universes.<br /><br />Everyone in The Land believes that Covenant is the "White Gold Wielder" who carries power strong enough to break the arch or to stop Foul. This sets up the fantasy/action story, where the people of The Land try to convince Covenant to learn to use his powers and to use them for the greater good and Foul tries to get the power.<br /><br />Since Covenant only visits The Land when he's suffered a head injury or some other strange type of sleep, he believes The Land is a fantasy of his own imagination. <br /><br />The people in The Land don't understand why he doesn't believe in them, but they do see how broken he himself is and realize that he won't be able to help them unless he heals from his personal traumas and builds some positive self-concept. <br /><br />It's a strange twist on the "apprentice who just needs confidence" trope and his mistakes during the early period are all the more appalling because he doesn't feel guilty.<br /><br />Throughout the book, there is not one scene in The Land that doesn't include Covenant. At the end, we are left with no proof one way or another that The Land is a real alternate world where Covenant travelled. Perhaps his Unbelief at the beginning was correct. The existential element of the story asks why we care which is real; he does what he does and the morality comes from his making the choices.<br /><br />That's heady stuff for a lightning-bolt-slinging fantasy series and it's not for everyone. It isn't modern "dark fantasy" with supposed anti-heroes who are just ashamed of being good at heart, it's real fantasy where the <i>character development</i> is on as epic a scale as the good-vs-evil plot. <br /><br />This isn't bus reading and no book is right for everyone, but it's an audacious and brilliant series that flexes fantasy's power to drive character. It's also a fun read with the single scariest magical nasties in the genre (the Reavers).<br /><br />The second series? It's different on the moral tale while following the same general plot arc, showing how the character involved changes the story. Not worth reading for everyone, but worth a shot if you like the first or if you are interested in the structure of fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4492091834359006514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_534219868" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/534219868" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/534219868?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/16665779-the-chronicles-of-thomas-covenant-the-unbeliever">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer534219868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer59574311306991558"><i>THE FOLLOWING CONTAINS SPOILAGE UP THE YING-YANG.</i><br/><br/>It's unfortunate that Donaldson opted to conclude the <i>First Chronicles of Thomas Covenant</i> as he did—for it reduced the showdown between the titular protagonist and Lord Foul the Despiser, which <i>should</i> have been epic, to one bordering upon the ridiculous. Indeed, I would have to look to David Eddings' <i>reductio ad absurdum</i>—in which the evil god Torak is defeated, more or less, by (Bel)Garion taunting him with the fact that <i>Ye'll never be loved, gasb</i></span>
  <span id="freeText59574311306991558" style="display:none"><i>THE FOLLOWING CONTAINS SPOILAGE UP THE YING-YANG.</i><br /><br />It's unfortunate that Donaldson opted to conclude the <i>First Chronicles of Thomas Covenant</i> as he did—for it reduced the showdown between the titular protagonist and Lord Foul the Despiser, which <i>should</i> have been epic, to one bordering upon the ridiculous. Indeed, I would have to look to David Eddings' <i>reductio ad absurdum</i>—in which the evil god Torak is defeated, more or less, by (Bel)Garion taunting him with the fact that <i>Ye'll never be loved, gasbag! Nyaahh!</i>—to find a greater disappointment in the endgame of a multi-volume work of fantasy. The spectral presence of personal favorites, like Foamfollower and Mhoram, went a ways towards alleviating my disappointment; but it was only modulated—a disappointment it remained. Fortunately, in the <i>Second Chronicles</i>, to which the character of Linden Avery was enjoined with TC, Donaldson's climax got <i>everything</i> right; and so, considering the pair of trilogies as one cohesive unit, it's the third best such series that I have ever read. Split them in twain? The second, though stronger, cannot match the pure wonder and introductory thrill that was to be found in the opener. <i>Lord Foul's Bane</i>, <i>The Illearth War</i>, and <i>The Power That Preserves</i> rocked my youthful world and were powerful evidence that Tolkien did not, in fact, have the final word to say in the field of <i>heroic</i> fantasy.<br /><br />Perhaps <i>anti-</i>heroic applies more aptly here—for Thomas Covenant is one peculiar choice for the redeemer of an idealistic world from the nihilistic designs of hatred incarnate. In the real world, Covenant was an author with a happy marriage and a newborn child who was diagnosed with leprosy, resulting in the amputation of two of the fingers on his right hand, and the cleaving of his family life as wife absconded with child out of fear of contagion. Embittered, reclusive, Covenant learns a series of mantras and rituals that will allow him to survive his disease—and also prove pretty darn handy when, upon suffering a series of accidents, he is <i>summoned</i> to a mysterious earthen realm known as <i>The Land</i>, an idealized version of our Earth, wherein wood and stone are vessels of an innate <i>Earthpower</i> that can be accessed by humans with the requisite skills. Covenant discovers that therein he is regarded as the reincarnation of Berek Halfhand, first of the High Lords who ordered the affairs of humans within the Land in harmony to the <i>Earthpower</i> of living Nature, defeated the insidious destructive influence of Lord Foul, and received the friendship/allegiance of the stone-skilled, expert-sailor Giants, the <i>Haruchai</i>, who, in the Bloodguard, form a personal, death-defying corps of protectors for the High Lords, and the <i>Ranyhyn</i>, great horses of the plains. Alas, Lord Foul is a timeless spirit, one who reflects the collective self-hatred and -loathing that exists, in some measure, within every man, and is thus impervious to actual death. After unleashing despair among the High Lords, Lord Foul convinced High Lord Kevin to invoke the Ritual of Desecration which, as apocalyptic rites undertaken in desperation are wont to do, fractured the Land but good. Lore, lives, and love was lost—and now, with the diminished remnant of the High Lords tentatively ruling from their Keep in Revelstoke, it appears that Lord Foul, ensconced within his Creche of a lair, is once again making a play to enact the utter destruction of the Land and engender his escape from imprisonment within the Arch of Time.<br /><br />Sound familiar? There are some fairly standard fantasy tropes at play within <i>The Chronicles</i>: what makes it work so well are Donaldson's fertile imagination; his blending of European mythology with that of the Orient; his rich vocabulary and confident writing style married to his dark, bleak themes; and, most of all, his creation of Covenant, a thoroughly unique and unlikeable figure who is thrust into a position of power and responsibility that he neither desires nor believes in. Indeed, refusing to accept the regeneration of his nerves when he awakens in the Land, his first act, one of violent, lustful negation, is the rape of Lena, a young Stonedowner girl. From this abhorrent, impulsive response is set in chain a cataclysmic series of events and currents that will play out across the trilogy: notwithstanding the guilt that Covenant bears for this gentle, damaged woman from that point after, if affects his standing as the reincarnation. With his wedding ring revealed as a puissant artifact of White Gold, the source of the Wild Magic that not even Lord Foul can defy, Covenant must make his way to Revelstoke, announce his existence to the High Lords, and undertake to defeat a dark enemy who stands revealed, more and more, as potentially an avatar of the part of Covenant that loathes all that his diseased self has become: a leper, an outcast, <i>unclean</i>, a man who drove the woman who loved him to flee in horror from his disfigured form. Guilt is the primary fuel for this trilogy's engine, and not just as engendered and driving within Covenant himself. It spreads from his malformed extremity like the streams of the Wild Fire he allegedly controls—and in the spectacular <i>Illearth War</i>, the second and strongest of the books, it is revealed that the dread tendrils of corruption may reach even unto such stalwarts as the Giants and the Bloodguard. Things will get messy and nasty ere the great war brewing has been settled by the expulsion of Foul's armies from the stricken realms of the Land.<br /><br />Fortunately for Covenant, at heart he is not a bad man. He pays for his violation, and pays dearly. He also befriends the Giant Saltheart Foamfollower, easily the best character in the trilogy, as well as (High) Lord Mhoram, a lore-rich member of the Revelstoke rulers who has the most understanding of this unusual, six-fingered individual who frequently raves and rages and treats those who strive to assist him with a ragged refusal to believe. And while, in lesser hands, Covenant's distancing from the Land could become a tiresome and overplayed trope, Donaldson incorporates it adeptly within Covenant's diseased life and loss back on Earth, together with his remorse for assaulting Lena and the mounting toll of lives claimed in service to what he supposedly represents and refuses to accept. And then there's Lord Foul, who, right up until that regrettable conclusion, is a wickedly rendered figure, oozing contempt, speaking pure venom, lashing the beings he hates beyond measure with eyes of coruscating despite. Foul is assisted by the <i>Ravers</i>—<i>turiya</i> Herem, <i>moksha</i> Jehannum, <i>samadhi</i> Sheol—a trio of brotherly spirits of a similar bent to their master, seemingly born with an animosity to the living trees of the land, and, through their capacity for possessing fleshly beings, capable of wreaking subtle and long-undetected harm as the Despiser maneuvers Covenant towards that final confrontation. Together with wholly unique creations like the <i>Ur-viles</i>, <i>Demondim</i>, and <i>Waynhim</i>, Donaldson imagined a fantastically inveigling supporting crew for the three books, ones that span a vast period of time within the Land during the interludes when Covenant (at the end of each volume) has found himself returned to his leprous self in our reality. Furthermore, that the entirety of the Land is not, in fact, a richly detailed, heatedly psychological instantiation of the currents that fuel his dreams is something that is never <i>quite</i> settled to the reader's satisfaction. But what matter that kind of truth? Whether figment of the imagination, or Platonically idealized realm of higher earthen existence, the Chronicles, as they unfold across that Land, are fast-moving, fully-fleshed and -formed, and magical, mystical, and marvelous enough to stretch the boundaries of the fantasy genre even while enriching everything about it that its fans love and require. IMO, the end result—and including the sequel trilogy—ranks below Tolkien and Wolfe only as the best such type of story I have ever read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="59574311306991558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_671029358" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/671029358" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/671029358?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer671029358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7949088592468711514">This series, famed for its vocabulary, offers a twist on the familiar person transported into an alternative fantasy existence. Here instead of the usual blasé acceptance the hero can't believe what has happened to them and won't believe what has happened to them. The doubt is established that everything might be a figment of the hero's imagination. The author encourages the doubt by having each of the hero's three visits to the fantastical 'The Land' precipitated each time by an accident in whi</span>
  <span id="freeText7949088592468711514" style="display:none">This series, famed for its vocabulary, offers a twist on the familiar person transported into an alternative fantasy existence. Here instead of the usual blasé acceptance the hero can't believe what has happened to them and won't believe what has happened to them. The doubt is established that everything might be a figment of the hero's imagination. The author encourages the doubt by having each of the hero's three visits to the fantastical 'The Land' precipitated each time by an accident in which the hero looses consciousness. This game with the reader was spoiled by the publication of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/596519.Gilden_Fire" title="Gilden-Fire by Stephen R. Donaldson" rel="nofollow noopener">Gilden Fire</a> which was written from the point of view of one of the denizens of 'The Land', but there you go. <br /><br />Those three, eventually four, visits are quite nice in that things in this other world steadily get worse, the emotional direction of the series tends downwards with brief remissions of humour.<br /><br />In addition to the real world character who, reasonably enough, doesn't believe what he's seeing the backdrop features a good creator who can't intervene in his creation without destroying it and is therefore obliged to use the hero, Thomas Covenant, as his tool or champion and the traditional Dark Lord who is trying to destroy everything because that's what he does. This furthers the idea that the whole thing is a figment of the hero's imagination and simply represents two aspects of his personality fighting it out during periods of unconsciousness.<br /><br />In the final volume the evil Dark Lord was defeated and the series was resolved. But then Donaldson brought back his earlier characters to create a second series. And then a third. This rather removes the threat of danger or the pleasure of resolution from the reading.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7949088592468711514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_290005121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/290005121" />
    <a title="Jack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8070402-jack"><img alt="Jack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1331005679p2/8070402.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/290005121?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack" href="/user/show/8070402-jack">Jack</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer290005121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8323407376504357553">Existentialist fantasy. <br/>Cleverly written. <br/>Brutal and dark. <br/><br/>That's all you really need to know before tackling these novels. That and the fact that this is one of the finest trilogy's ever penned, period. <br/><br/>At a surface level, these fantasy novels would appear as nothing out of the ordinary - outcast get's cast off into a fantasy world, is the only one who can save the land, is destined to destroy great evil threatening it yada yada. But Thomas Covenant is a bitter leper, and refuses to acknowledge</span>
  <span id="freeText8323407376504357553" style="display:none">Existentialist fantasy. <br />Cleverly written. <br />Brutal and dark. <br /><br />That's all you really need to know before tackling these novels. That and the fact that this is one of the finest trilogy's ever penned, period. <br /><br />At a surface level, these fantasy novels would appear as nothing out of the ordinary - outcast get's cast off into a fantasy world, is the only one who can save the land, is destined to destroy great evil threatening it yada yada. But Thomas Covenant is a bitter leper, and refuses to acknowledge the land a real because to do so would lead to the end of his own sanity... <br /><br />It is this manipulation of fantasy conventions that Donaldson employs to creative a novel of intricate paradoxes and intelligent commentaries on how we deal with pain in our lives - and the cost dwelling on our misfortunes. Thomas Covenant is a despicable character, but his plight, and more importantly the lands is a compelling one. <br /><br />Of course, all this wouldn't matter if the story was weak. Lord Foul's Bane (the first of the trilogy) falters a little in this department, it lingers to long on certain aspects, and Covenant is frustrating to the point of wanting to make you throw the book across the room. But it is worth persisting, because the two novels that follow (The Illearth War, The Power that Preserves) are incredible. The scope and charters really come into their own - far from the cliche's that they originally appeared to be. Quite frankly, some of the moments in these two books surpass almost anything else I have ever read, their passion is truly inspiring. The battle scenes that fill the books are also worth mentioning, unique and well written, they surpass many of their peers, putting great wars like those in Lord of the Rings to shame. <br /><br />However, this may not be for everyone. As I said, the first book is weak compared the rest of the trilogy and contains a scene near the start so repulsive that it might deter some readers from persisting. Furthermore, Donaldson's operatic style and elaborate language might also annoy some. Finally, the brutality may be just too much for some readers, at it's darkest moments it will leave you feeling gutted in ways that other fantasy books skimpily don't. <br /><br />In a genre full of happy endings, loveable heroes, simplistic plots and themes no deeper than good and evil "The Chronicles of Thomas Covenant" sticks out like a sore thumb. It transcends it's genre, and firmly places itself amongst the ranks of one of the greatest series ever written. It might turn some readers off but there is no denying this is nothing short of a masterpiece. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8323407376504357553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_721511498" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/721511498" />
    <a title="Tim Hicks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111076-tim-hicks"><img alt="Tim Hicks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1327258501p2/111076.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/721511498?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim Hicks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim Hicks" href="/user/show/111076-tim-hicks">Tim Hicks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111076-tim-hicks?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer721511498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1506168272931242301">I read this years ago, but I found an old note to self. All you really need to know about this series is that in the 13-page first chapter of the first book, Donaldson uses these words: spavined, desuetude, turgid, condign, gratuitous, dyspeptic, melanoma, immedicable, improvident, lambent, immanence, celerity, preterite, abnegation, carious, and exudation. Now I have a 99th-percentile vocabulary, and it includes a lot of those words, but I know that most readers don't and I am sure that Donalds</span>
  <span id="freeText1506168272931242301" style="display:none">I read this years ago, but I found an old note to self. All you really need to know about this series is that in the 13-page first chapter of the first book, Donaldson uses these words: spavined, desuetude, turgid, condign, gratuitous, dyspeptic, melanoma, immedicable, improvident, lambent, immanence, celerity, preterite, abnegation, carious, and exudation. Now I have a 99th-percentile vocabulary, and it includes a lot of those words, but I know that most readers don't and I am sure that Donaldson was aware of that. <br /> <br />Later on, Donaldson chucks in words that aren't even in my two-volume Shorter Oxford Dictionary. Telic, cymar, jerrids, ... <br /><br />In chapter four, I laughed out loud when he wrote, "A grimace like a clench of intransigence knotted her mien." That's just silly. I am convinced that Donaldson wrote at least the first book on a drunken bet. Stir in the repulsive Eeyore of a hero and it's even more likely. <br /><br />If you haven't read this steaming pile of excrement, don't get sucked into wasting your time with it. Go get some Guy Gavriel Kay or Lois McMaster Bujold and read some GOOD fantasy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1506168272931242301" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1614640818" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1614640818" />
    <a title="A. Dawes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55066255-a-dawes"><img alt="A. Dawes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470731545p2/55066255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1614640818?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A. Dawes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A. Dawes" href="/user/show/55066255-a-dawes">A. Dawes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1614640818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17125038886530064733">I loved Donaldson's works, but whether I'd still love them is another story altogether. I read the first novel of his most recent return to the world a few years ago, and found it incredibly thin and dull. I won't read the sequel, but I'm secretly hoping that the original chronicles were as marvelous as I remembered them to be....<br/><br/>At the time I read the first trilogy, I'd just re-read Tolkien and was indulging in other high/epic fantasy novelists, which were popular at the time: Feist, Wurtz. Mcc</span>
  <span id="freeText17125038886530064733" style="display:none">I loved Donaldson's works, but whether I'd still love them is another story altogether. I read the first novel of his most recent return to the world a few years ago, and found it incredibly thin and dull. I won't read the sequel, but I'm secretly hoping that the original chronicles were as marvelous as I remembered them to be....<br /><br />At the time I read the first trilogy, I'd just re-read Tolkien and was indulging in other high/epic fantasy novelists, which were popular at the time: Feist, Wurtz. MccAffrey, Eddings, Brooks, Weis &amp; Hickman, and a few Forgotten Realms novels. Donaldson, however, felt the freshest and most capable of these writers (outside of Tolkien). The reason was that Donaldson successfully challenged conventions of the high fantasy genre by utilising a true anti-hero as the protagonist. Thomas Covenant isn't just a leper: he's bitter, self-loathing and also loathes society. He has lost all passion for life. His physical condition in a way, is a very vivid representation of his tortured soul. While the other fictional heroes at the time were at heart, good and just, Covenant's values were questionable for the most part. <br /><br />Donaldson handles the leprosy well. His father was a doctor in India who specialised in treating lepers, so the details feel authentic.<br /><br />Yet when Covenant the Leper is thrust into a fantastic world, where his physical scarring and symptoms vanish, he succumbs to the most unforgivable and heinous of acts, and commits rape. How can you support an anti-hero after that? Yet Donaldson manages it, and this bitter, twisted hero makes for one of the great characters of epic fantasy. In a way, I've only seen George RR Martin handle flawed anti-heroes as successfully (Jamie's rise from tossing Bran off the balcony).<br /><br />I'm hoping that these original chronicles are still being read. I'm hoping they haven't dated too much. The details and layers made it far more sophisticated than Eddings and Brooks at the time. I'm also hoping that Donaldson's latest additions to the series aren't reflective of the original novels - but you never know....It's all about blind hope really, so I'm not re-reading the original trilogy just in case it spoils the memories. <br /><br />Recommended for lovers of rich, tapestry-like fantasies with a dark edge. <br /><br /> <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="17125038886530064733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_13806080" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13806080" />
    <a title="Dantalion64" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/843253-dantalion64"><img alt="Dantalion64" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13806080?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dantalion64" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dantalion64" href="/user/show/843253-dantalion64">Dantalion64</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780739425480" href="/book/show/116064.The_First_Chronicles_of_Thomas_Covenant_the_Unbeliever">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13806080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15092928526630235684">Great, amazing books. Turns romantic, sylvan fantasy on its ear. Many people complain about these books because it moves slowly, or because the main character is reprehensible. The only thing I can say is “Deal.” When you have a series of books centering on the salvation of a lost and embittered man, he’s not going to start out being a nice guy! If you want your fantasy heroes to be handsome, valorous, strong, and virtuous, go re-read Tolkien. These books are not about saving the world from evil</span>
  <span id="freeText15092928526630235684" style="display:none">Great, amazing books. Turns romantic, sylvan fantasy on its ear. Many people complain about these books because it moves slowly, or because the main character is reprehensible. The only thing I can say is “Deal.” When you have a series of books centering on the salvation of a lost and embittered man, he’s not going to start out being a nice guy! If you want your fantasy heroes to be handsome, valorous, strong, and virtuous, go re-read Tolkien. These books are not about saving the world from evil; they’re really about pain, guilt, redemption, and the consequences of cleaving to one’s beliefs. They’re not for the faint of heart, and they aren’t light reading, but they’re an immensely satisfying, <i>adult</i> fantasy series. I've read these books at least a dozen times, and it's been a different experience each time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15092928526630235684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26715704" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26715704" />
    <a title="Katilian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/116292-katilian"><img alt="Katilian" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26715704?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katilian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katilian" href="/user/show/116292-katilian">Katilian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/116292-katilian?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/116292-katilian?shelf=library">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26715704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13747983942957310600">These books were a reread, although I was a teenager when I first encountered them and didn't remember too much of the plot. Honestly, I have admiration for the concept of this series more than I have liking for the actual books. On the one hand, you have a main character who is a complete jackass--probably the first antihero that I ever encountered, now that I think about it. It's not that he doesn't have a reason to be a jackass; he is, after all, battling leprosy. It's that he doesn't do it w</span>
  <span id="freeText13747983942957310600" style="display:none">These books were a reread, although I was a teenager when I first encountered them and didn't remember too much of the plot. Honestly, I have admiration for the concept of this series more than I have liking for the actual books. On the one hand, you have a main character who is a complete jackass--probably the first antihero that I ever encountered, now that I think about it. It's not that he doesn't have a reason to be a jackass; he is, after all, battling leprosy. It's that he doesn't do it with any grace, that he doesn't deal with the admittedly bizarre situation that he finds himself in, that he spends every second thinking of himself only, that he has more mood swings than a teenage girl... he's hard to deal with. Add to that Donaldson's swallowing a thesaurus and tendency not to either show nor tell enough, and you'd think you had a disaster on your hands.<br /><br />On the other hand, you have a fascinating premise: the main character is a leper, he's brought into a beautiful, magical world, he's impossibly healed, and he doesn't believe a bit of it. He's the main hope of a group of people who don't, as far as he's concerned, really exist. And those people are the ones that draw me in, make me want to read about them: Lord Mhoram, who is both noble and totally human; Saltheart Foamfollower, the loyal giant who struggles with his desire to turn against what he believes in; Hile Troy, a man from our world who loves the Land more than anything; Bannor, the unsleeping guardsman who constantly saves Covenant's life, much to Covenant's dismay. These people and more, plus the fascinating world of the Land, make the books worth reading even when you want to kick Covenant's backside and suggest he grow up a little.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13747983942957310600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_66690974" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66690974" />
    <a title="Tessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1692347-tessa"><img alt="Tessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246141361p2/1692347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/66690974?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tessa" href="/user/show/1692347-tessa">Tessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66690974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3560257900941868610">I am one of those who love love love this series, and I think one of the reasons is that it is so dense and unappealing. We don't like the main character, but we're not meant to, we can't really understand the Land because of barriers put up and it's inherent strangeness but then there are the parts that linger, the phrases and ideas, you find yourself mouthing along as Foamfollower repeats "joy is in the ears that hear" or Mhoram's dread declaration that "in dreams i hear him laughing"<br/><br/>the story</span>
  <span id="freeText3560257900941868610" style="display:none">I am one of those who love love love this series, and I think one of the reasons is that it is so dense and unappealing. We don't like the main character, but we're not meant to, we can't really understand the Land because of barriers put up and it's inherent strangeness but then there are the parts that linger, the phrases and ideas, you find yourself mouthing along as Foamfollower repeats "joy is in the ears that hear" or Mhoram's dread declaration that "in dreams i hear him laughing"<br /><br />the story is reasonably simple, following a bizarre altercation with a beggar Covenant is knocked down by a police car, but wakes up in a place called the Land where a shadowy ominous figure gives him a message, either you can help me now and I'll attack you in 49 years, giving you plenty of time to prepare and still lose, or you can be defeated now by this lesser evil.<br />Covenant believes the whole thing to be a dream at first but later maintains that the Land cannot be real even when it's determined to prove otherwise. But one thing Donaldson never does is actually say whether or not the Land is real or a figment of Covenant's authorial imagination. The Land wants him not to experience guilt, because dear god does he feel guilty for everything, including at one point walking on grass, but to accept that just because he is ill that he is not powerless.<br />The first time you read it it's about the story and you want to slap Covenant and shout at him and say look at the world you're allowing to be destroyed.<br />The second time it's about the twists you missed the first time, Lord Foul's sneaky machinations and the Ravers, the desolation of the Bloodguard "womanless, ascetic, and old" and the slow desperation of the Lords, even when you understand Covenant's enforced antipathy.<br />The third time it's about the language, the way he fits words together, although I'll be the first to admit he often uses $10 words when something simple would be just as effective, there is a scene about being left untransmogrified that had me scrabbling for my dictionary, and feeling cheated. It's Elena's "look of apocalypse" and the "sense of wrongness" and "joy is in the ears that hear," <br />I know why people hate it, I'm not the world's biggest fan of the second series, finding it inferior to this one, and I'm holding off on the third until it's finished, but for once it's not a fantasy book about a perfect hero, or Tolkien and his trees, it's a world we feel the lack of when we close the book.<br /><br />i think it's best to let the book sell itself<br /><br />" "We have beauty, too. We call it 'scenery'"<br /><br />"'Scenery.'" Mhoram echoed. "The word is strange to me--but I do not like the sound."<br /><br />Covenant felt oddly shaken, as if he has just looked over his shoulder and found himself standing too close to a precipice. "It means that beauty is something extra, " he rasped. "It's nice, but we can live without it."<br /><br />"Without?" Mhoram's gaze glittered dangerously.<br /><br />And from behind him Foamfollower breathed in ashtonishment, "Life without beauty? Ah, my friend! How do you resist despair?""</span>
  <a data-text-id="3560257900941868610" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_94362944" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94362944" />
    <a title="Richard Benoit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2469271-richard-benoit"><img alt="Richard Benoit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320188466p2/2469271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94362944?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Benoit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Benoit" href="/user/show/2469271-richard-benoit">Richard Benoit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94362944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8308849043932850904">When I first read all the glowing reviews for this series, I thought I had found a winner. I purchased all of the first three books of the initial trilogy. I assumed with so many great recommendations I couldn't go wrong. Boy was I wrong! The hero?, Thomas Covenant ( a leper), whines, cries and rages his way thru page after page after page. Covenant is one of the most despicable characters I have ever come upon. A quarter of the way into the book a young, innocent girl of 16 tries to befriend Co</span>
  <span id="freeText8308849043932850904" style="display:none">When I first read all the glowing reviews for this series, I thought I had found a winner. I purchased all of the first three books of the initial trilogy. I assumed with so many great recommendations I couldn't go wrong. Boy was I wrong! The hero?, Thomas Covenant ( a leper), whines, cries and rages his way thru page after page after page. Covenant is one of the most despicable characters I have ever come upon. A quarter of the way into the book a young, innocent girl of 16 tries to befriend Covenant, takes him to her home to eat and meet her family, and he repays her by losing his temper and raping her. At this point I threw the book down and refused to read anymore. The image of a leper molesting a child was too much for me. After a few days I decided that something must radically change in the plot because the book has so many good reviews. There must be some redeeming change in his character to make him worthy of being the hero. So I decided to read the rest of it. I shouldn't have bothered. Covenant doesn't alter his behavior one bit. He whines, he cries, he argues, he rages. When attacked, his first inclination is to hide and cower. He doesn't find the will to fight until he is so terrified that he loses his temper and slaughters his assailant. And then he's ashamed of himself rages some more because he's been forced to kill. I found myself rooting for the bad guys, hoping one of them would lop his head off. That way the story could continue without him in it. I threw the book in the trash along with parts 2 &amp; 3 unread. I won't have that kind of drivel on my bookshelf. Someone might come across it and think I enjoyed it! If you enjoy fantasy that doesn't follow the usual tired and overused plot lines, you should check out a series by Kate Elliot called "Crown Of Stars". It's everything intelligent fantasy should be.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8308849043932850904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1944196266" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1944196266" />
    <a title="Ana " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5237420-ana"><img alt="Ana " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573472177p2/5237420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1944196266?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana " href="/user/show/5237420-ana">Ana </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=about-murders">about-murders</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=page-turner">page-turner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=me-likey-a-lot">me-likey-a-lot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=magic">magic</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=creatures-spirits">creatures-spirits</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=girls-kick-ass">girls-kick-ass</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1944196266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6202664775189320091">More like 3.5 stars, but I do have a soft spot for classical fantasy... <br/><br/>So. Lemme vent. Covenant is an almost impossible to like character. He is sick, weak, a rapist, a coward, fearful, disdainful and has almost no care or understanding for other people apart from himself. And that's just the first 70 pages of the book... But, you know, I grew rather fond of him over the course of the first three books. He is a complete anti-hero. First of all, he suffers from leprosy: if there is another way i</span>
  <span id="freeText6202664775189320091" style="display:none">More like 3.5 stars, but I do have a soft spot for classical fantasy... <br /><br />So. Lemme vent. Covenant is an almost impossible to like character. He is sick, weak, a rapist, a coward, fearful, disdainful and has almost no care or understanding for other people apart from himself. And that's just the first 70 pages of the book... But, you know, I grew rather fond of him over the course of the first three books. He is a complete anti-hero. First of all, he suffers from leprosy: if there is another way in which to describe a character as "dirty-from-the-inside", I know not of it. Second of all, he genuinely rapes a fifteen year old virgin a day after he met her inside of his "dream" - this other world called The Land, where the Earth actually heals you. And then throughout the books, people die for him, sacrifice themselves for him, offer him the gift of their own existence, all while he still firmly believes he is dreaming of a fantasy world and it's all inside of his head. It is genuinely difficult to find any sympathy for him: he keeps making stupid mistakes, keeps misunderstanding signs, keeps being a coward over and over and over again. His thought process might just drive you mad. And yet, despite all of this, he is exactly the kind of character I love reading about. He is complex, he fights for his own survival, he makes human mistakes because he is the embodiment of human fear and somehow, takes you through all the important feelings and emotions that humans go through. So yeah, I quite like Thomas Covenant. He's a dick, but I like him. <br /><br />The writing has been hailed to match Tolkien's - I can't agree. Yes, this is an epic fantasy, a classical fantasy, the kind where you have maps at the beginning of the book, but it doesn't touch on the might that "Lord of the Rings" bears. It's complex, though not complex enough. And the writing can be at times blunt, though it keeps a steady pace throughout. <br /><br />Is it too long? Maybe. But is any other fantasy saga too long? Probably. All of them contain thousands of pages of story, but that is part of the magic that fantasy books give you: so much world in which to get so absolutely lost. This volume, which contains the first three books, is 1150 pages long, and I have read it in three days. That tells you a lot about my willingness to avoid my responsibilities, as well as how gripping this work still is. I keep wanting to know what's going to happen next. <br /><br />Should you read it? Yes, if you're a fantasy fan: magic and nonexistent lands, Giants and monsters, if all of these things touch your imagination, you maybe should. No, if you hate fantasy and have no patience for diseased characters and rotten decisions. You decide. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6202664775189320091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5045643489417844930">I read this in high school, and it made quite an impression. <br/><br/>I liked it very much. It has big bones, like Tolkein's work, but is more gritty, more morally complex. <br/><br/>The main character is a strong anti-hero (something I had not encountered much before), deeply flawed, and the structure of the alter-universe lends itself well to the notion of a free-will world. <br/><br/>Like a darker, infinitely more grown-up cousin of The Chronicles of Narnia, this lends itself well to discussion of theology. However, it s</span>
  <span id="freeText5045643489417844930" style="display:none">I read this in high school, and it made quite an impression. <br /><br />I liked it very much. It has big bones, like Tolkein's work, but is more gritty, more morally complex. <br /><br />The main character is a strong anti-hero (something I had not encountered much before), deeply flawed, and the structure of the alter-universe lends itself well to the notion of a free-will world. <br /><br />Like a darker, infinitely more grown-up cousin of The Chronicles of Narnia, this lends itself well to discussion of theology. However, it stands on its own, and you have to look to find that aspect. <br /><br />Upon graduation, I left "The Land, to those who know it" in the senior year "Will and Prophesy". <br />Sadly, my handwriting was not that great, and it was published as "The HAND, to those who know it." Not the same, even a little. <br /><br />Donaldson wrote another trilogy in this world, but I did not take to it as I did to the first three books. Enjoy the first three, and see the ideas and world creep into your mental vocabulary. </span>
